This is a great book to help the reader understand people of other cultures by seeking to understand their values and the expression of those values in comparison to those of the reader.  Being able to see the values of others aids in bringing people together to create a healthy living environment by reducing the conflicts often manifest in value expressions. This book was a required reading in a Cultural Anthropology Class and an enjoyable book to read.  I highly recommend it to people of all Religions and Cultures.

B004QL7K6G|3.0|While it does not reach the apex of bizarro comedic genius attained by David Wain's cult classic Wet Hot American Summer, Wanderlust, penned by Wain and Ken Marino (Party Down), inspires enough laughs to allow its tepid plot to be overlooked, due in large part to the committed performances of its leads--notably Rudd, whose sexual-pump-up speech is one for the ages, proving that simple anatomy can be ridiculously funny when flowing from the right mouth. Other actors, like Marino, seem annoyingly misplaced and more than a few gags are clumsy, but ultimately this shaggy dog of a film rears its head in profane, gleeful confusion enough times to endear itself to you. Devotees of the cerebral and short-lived Stella will rejoice.<br /> The wavering plot concerns George (Rudd) and Linda (Aniston), two New York up-and-comers whose lives are derailed by virtually spontaneous unemployment. The two have recently purchased a miniature apartment referred to as both a \\"studio\\" and \\"micro-loft,\\" depending on the whims of their avaricious and unhinged realtor. The apartment is the sort of place where the bed drops down from the wall and the addition of both a couch and an armchair would result in over-crowding. The New York scenes resound with satirical validity and are over perhaps too soon, resulting in a quasi-road picture vs. hippie-commune subplot that gradually grows to assimilate all other story threads. Pacing is Wanderlust's general flaw, with several scenes running too long--particularly those at Ken Marino's suburban hell castle which could have been handled during less intrusive (and plot-stalling) telephone conversations since watching grown men prance about smashing plates to express their ennui and women drinking into oblivion have been beaten to death and beyond in lesser movies.<br /> Wanderlust flounders when the action is confined to the hippie commune. Many subplots jump in and out including one in which a thoroughly gaunt and wheelchair bound Alan Alda attemptsto remember where he kept the deed to the commune, as vague land developers appear to have their eye on repossessing it. The best scenes are the ones which seem hijacked by David Wain and rise above cliche set-ups. Most of these include a hardly recognizable Justin Theroux whose attempts to free George's mind during an arm-swinging yoga session results in fabulous wordplay from Rudd. Funnier than Aniston up in a tree on DMT, which could have been handled better (though the hallucinations were amusing). Elsewhere, the film is enlivened by character actors like Joe Lo Truglio (he was the \\"you guys on myspace?\\" freak from Superbad and the guy with the weird voice from I Love You, Man) whose novelist character Wayne spends much more time running amok with his manhood exposed than he does writing his novel about \\"The Monster of the Beltway\\". Ultimately, Wanderlust flows in a typically deranged Wain manner with cars showing up in odd places, babies delivered at inopportune moments (and with gory results) and polygamy taking hold of the central plot. There is even some Stella-reminiscent action later on, once the tone of the film has been thrown headlong into madcap territory from which it emerges and drowns, emerges and drowns, dragging itself at last toward a smug, enjoyable finish.

B0059XTUXQ|4.0|Reviews that have criticized The Iron Lady for being a muddled patchwork of segments in the political life of Margaret Thatcher, scenes that took place without a context, have missed the point of the film.  This is not really a straight-on biopic of Margaret Thatcher, and thank goodness as I don't think I could have sat through a two and a half hour political film.  What it is, though, is a story about this larger than life political figure in her declining years whose mind is taking her back to various points in her life...as her memories come to her in segmented scenes, so does the movie.  True, I did learn more about Margaret Thatcher the politician than I had known; but more, I reveled in the story of a lonely, isolated woman who has kept company with her fragmented memories which included her relationship with her now deceased husband, her rise to power, and various scenes of her life in Parliament.  That Meryl Streep embodies Margaret Thatcher is an understatement, her performance is amazing, and her third Oscar win was truly deserved. But the movie's point of view is Thatcher's, and the movie-goer should watch this film through her eyes.

0393338398|4.0|If you're a true baseball enthusiast, there's a reasonable chance you enjoy statistics.  Or at least, you're not afraid of them.  Michael Lewis is certainly counting on this fact from his target audience, because while half of Moneyball is a story about Billy Beane and the Oakland Athletics, the other half is an often repetitive tale of the history of baseball statistics and scouting.<br /><br />I would say about 75% of this book is well written and insightful.  You get a thorough story of Billy Beane as the GM of the Oakland A's, but equally importantly you get his back story which explains how he came to be the man he is.  You get an understanding of how the sport of baseball was run before Billy Beane's impact as a GM.  You get the underdog story of a bunch of players whose hopes were all but lost, coming together to accomplish something incredible, even if it doesn't end in a championship.  When the book is good, it's great.<br /><br />Lewis only has one real stumbling point, as far as I'm concerned--he can repeat himself to drive a point home, well after the point is understood.  One of the key aspects of this book is looking into the history of baseball statistics, involving the writing of Bill James and the various organizations that started to analyze the game better than Major League Baseball did itself.  That's great, but there were times when Lewis simply kept writing when he didn't need to.  There were chapters that I'd start to read, and think, \\"Am I in the wrong place?  I'm pretty sure I read this exact same fact already...\\"<br /><br />Now, I'm a baseball enthusiast.  I'm a fan of math and statistical analysis.  I loved the overview of how these organizations were changing baseball statistics as we know them.  But when you're repeating the same statistics, or fawning over them in the same fashion, it can start to cause weariness.  That's what I felt, in some of the chapters Lewis presented on the statistics of baseball.  I'm not saying they weren't great; I loved them.  I only loved them, however, until I felt like I was reading them twice.<br /><br />Overall, this is a book I cannot strongly enough recommend to any baseball fan who's not deterred by the statistics that drive it.  It's a story of a man who didn't accept the norms just because it was the standard.  He challenged his team with success in the face of teams with higher payrolls by trying to reinvent scouting in Major League Baseball.  Whether or not you agree that he and Paul DePodesta found success in their methods, this is a book worth reading.  In 2002 I had a narrower view of baseball, being a grade school child rooting for one team and knowing little about statistics or any other teams.  Now that I can appreciate these facets of the baseball, I can't recommend this book more to anyone interested in the sport.at; I loved them. The Social Network was a great debut for Reznor but the score obviously lacked a lot even though the praise it got states otherwise. On the Blu-ray it was revealed that Reznor and Ross didn't score to picture and it was up to Fincher and his editor to craft a score out of the music that was produced. Now, I don't know what the process was this time but I would say they paid more attention to scene structure. A lot of scenes were edited well to the music, but then again the music could have been composed beforehand. Hopefully a featurette on the Blu-ray will reveal  the process.<br /><br />For what is included here there is a lot to be experienced, and more of it borders on sound design than say melodic or thematic music. This is a fully synthesized score that blends textures, tones, drones and atmospheres to craft certain emotional states. Any traditional score fan or traditional musician might scoff at this score, but the meticulous execution of it all gives everything an immense precision. I think any fan of Akira Yamaoka's scores to the Silent Hill games will appreciate this score immensely. It is dark and gritty and very ambient. The music will make you uncomfortable and unsettle you, but as a complete experience is strangely unique. While I can't say the music is as accessible as The Social Network it definitely absorbed me into a bizarre state of mind for the duration of the listen.<br /><br />This is a monster of a score that needs to be experienced, and I think everybody will take something different away from it. I can see a lot of people hating it and I can see a lot of people loving it. I do love it, and it works immensely well to the picture. It's definitely not a 1-note score but rather a very dynamic experience with multiple layers. The 3 CD's are worth going through as the layers peel away like an onion the deeper and deeper you go. 1451627289|5.0|Stephen King<br />11/22/63<br />Hardcover<br />Publisher: Scribner<br />Publication Date: November 8, 2011<br />ISBN-13: 978-1451627282<br />849 pages<br /><br />  I've always been a fan of Stephen King's work. However, I don't normally read a lot of horror, don't really enjoy being frightened out of my wits, and would much rather spend my time reading Science Fiction, Fantasy, or Poetry instead. But, there is always something compelling and interesting about every work by this grand master of dark fiction and I seek out and buy his new books whenever they are released. A few stand out to me as some of the most interesting books I've ever read. The Stand, Under the Dome, and the Dark Tower series for instance, will always be some of the most enjoyable reading I've ever done. But 11/22/63 stands alone among all of Stephen King's other works. Yes, it is as interesting and persuasive as anything he's ever written. Yes, he is the \\"everyman\\" of modern writers and proves it every time he sits down at his typewriter, including this time. Yes, he knows his audience and writes accordingly. But the assassination of John F. Kennedy still tugs at the hearts and minds of those who lived through those unhappy times, 11/22/63 even more so. King does not tell us what was lost on that fateful day in Dallas. He deftly, heartbreakingly shows us in infinite detail. The twists and turns found in this unique time-travel tale about the prevention of the assassination of JFK made my head spin, but in a good way. The consequences of time travel are clearly spelled out in King's alternate universe and the myriad of possible time-lines and \\"what-ifs\\" are indelibly written. What if you could travel to the past, stay as long as you wanted, and upon your return find that only two minutes had passed? What would you do with all that time? What would you do if you traveled to the past and found out that you had somehow become the world's worst enemy? Or that you had sufficiently changed the course of time to cause a near-extinction event?<br /><br />  The protagonist, Jacob \\"Jake\\" Epping, a recently-divorced high school English teacher, continues down the time-stream handed to him by a dying time traveler and attempts to prevent JFK's murder. But the machinations of time are not easily deceived and the past has a few surprises in store for anyone arrogant enough to try and change it. Some might even say, \\"Be careful what you wish for!\\" Yet, King maneuvers through this mine-field of twisting time-lines as deftly as any writer who has ever attempted to manipulate the past and the various sub-plots and side-stories are perfect accompaniments to the story itself. We learn early on that changing the past, even in small increments, has significant consequences. So, what do you imagine an enormous change to the past might do? Don't worry; Stephen King has already done that for you...<br /><br />  I won't give away any of the surprises found in 11/22/63 but I will tell you that there are at least a dozen \\"wow\\" moments (and I mean WOW!) in this story and it is well worth the price of admission. You'll be changed by this book and you'll be filled with emotion. You will come away with your thoughts provoked and your feelings stirred. You will contemplate the seriousness of the story and you will be moved. You can't help but be.<br /><br />  11/22/63 is best suited for history buffs, time travel fans, and every other human on the planet. It's that good, folks, an instant classic, in my estimation, and I recommend it to readers of every genre imaginable, and even some unimaginable.<br /><br />  M-O-O-N that spells masterpiece!<br /><br />6 out of 5 stars<br /><br />The Alternative<br />Southeast Wisconsinof time to cause a near-extinction event?<br /><br />  The protagonist, Jacob \\"Jake\\" Epping, a recently-divorced high school English teacher, continues down the time-stream handed to him by a dying time traveler and attempts to prevent JFK's murder. 0156027321|5.0|***THIS REVIEW CONTAINS SPOILERS***<br /><br />I can see why there is such a range in opinions regarding this book. Reading others' reviews, I see how much some people despise it while others are completely moved by it.  I fall into the latter group - for me, something resonated deeply.<br /><br />I could probably spend 227 days replying to other people's reviews, especially those that didn't enjoy it; but I think what it comes down to is that you have to read this book as a philosophical, spiritual, and/or religious journey.  It IS about religion; or perhaps, more accurately, God.  It says so in the Author's Note \\"Then the elderly man said, 'I have a story that will make you believe in God.'\\"<br /><br />So, if you are not prepared to examine this story as an allegory from that perspective, then I suppose you won't really enjoy the book.  If I was to speculate as to a couple of reasons why someone would NOT enjoy this book, I would say that Part 1 is slow-moving.  There is a really BIG set-up in this book and the set-up is extremely important in understanding the rest of the book.  In particular, descriptions of zoo life, including the temperament and care of the zoo animals is important; as is Pi's devotion to God and practice of three different religions (i.e., Hindu, Muslim, and Christianity).<br /><br />For anyone thinking of reading this book or who has started and is having trouble moving forward...be patient.  Every piece of information from these early chapters contributes to the understanding of Parts 2 and 3.  If you are a person that likes to skip over parts, or likes to skip to the end, seriously...don't bother picking up this book.  It's totally not for you.  This book is about the journey.  I've read reviews saying that Parts 1, 2 and 3 are disjointed - I couldn't disagree more; in fact, I would argue that they are three necessary parts to the whole.<br /><br />**SPOILER ALERT***<br /><br />Another reason I think some people would NOT like this book is the ambiguous ending. The story told in Part 2 is absolutely riveting.  Martel is a brilliant story-teller.  I could envision myself in Pi's shoes and followed with the ups and downs in his psyche as he experienced them being shipwrecked. It would have been a really great story if the end had been Pi's (and Richard Parker's) rescue.  In fact, I bet that for those that didn't like the book, they would have given a higher rating if it had ended like that.  (Just guessing).  But, instead Martel offers an alternative account of Pi's experience (stripped down and much more horrific).  The ending thus can leave the reader puzzled, frustrated, or perhaps even angry (based on reading others' reviews).<br /><br />Most people tend to like nice tight and neat endings after investing so much time, emotion, and energy to reading a book.  Martel's story leaves us hanging and allows the reader to choose the ending they believe.  I still get goosebumps reading the text that brings it all together (from Chapter 99, p. 317):<br /><br />\\"'So tell me, since it makes no factual difference to you and you can't prove the question either way, which story do you prefer? Which is the better story, the story with animals or the story without animals?' Mr. Okamoto: 'That's an interesting question?' Mr. Chiba: 'The story with animals.' Mr. Okamoto: 'Yes. The story with animals is the better story.' Pi Patel: 'Thank you. And so it goes with God.'\\"<br /><br />Brilliant!!  This is the text that brings together all three parts of the book.  I love the way a few little sentences can pull together a whole story.  Love it!  This passage brings to mind the stories from the Old Testament, Daniel and the Lion's Den, Noah's Ark, the Tower of Babel, etc.  Those were some good stories, weren't they?<br />Ok, so at the end of the day, Martel's writing is superb, the story is absolutely riveting, and the ending is thought-provoking.  I can see myself reading this book in a few years with a fresh perspectiveand  re-interpreting the story.  It is THAT kind of book.and  re-interpreting the story.  It is THAT kind of book.

I absolutely love the album, it has helped me through some tough times in my life and holds a special place for me. But this box set is overpriced for what you get. The album is great, it has never sounded better, that is not an issue.<br />Is There Anybody Out There? is a fantastic glimpse into Pink Floyd as live band in the '80s and it's great to hear the entire album live with the original members. The live album also sounds fantastic, I have never owned this before so I don't know if it sounds better than before.<br />The demos were the main selling point for me, I had never heard any of these before and they really were very intriguing to hear the evolution of some of the songs. I liked the Roger Water's demos the most, they don't sound the best but they are very cool to hear how everything was already clearly mapped out in Water's head long before they ever went into the studio. My only complaint about the demos is that I wish they had included all of Roger's demos, but I understand that it would have been not the most entertaining thing to hear and it's probably for the best.<br />The DVD is a little underwhelming, but fairly enjoyable. Behind the Wall is very enjoyable and interesting, Another Brick in the Wall, Part 2 promotional video is boring and doesn't look very good, but the best part is the Earl's Court live clip. That does not include the books, the books are nice and I love them. The scarf is cheap and is basically falling apart at the seams, the coasters are cheap and flimsy, and the marbles are cheap and the bricks design rubs off if you play with them too rough or too much. The replica ticket is nice but unnecessary, I enjoy the Mark Fischer drawings, and the cards are fairly stupid as well since they don't offer insight or thoughts, just lyrics.<br />Overall, the main reason to buy this set is for the excellent new remasters and the demos in the best sounding incarnation on the market. But, this set could have been released for so much cheaper or they could have delayed it to include the 5.1 mix. I want to hear The Wall in 5.1, but I do not own an SACD player and probably never will. These Immersion sets are the only way that those of us without SACD can hear these albums in 5.1 in high quality audio, I highly doubt that EMI will release a stand alone blu-ray.<br /><br />There are a lot of things missing from this set and it's overpriced, but it does include a lot of worthwhile materials that Pink Floyd fans will enjoy. I enjoyed the majority of it and I will get many hours of listening from this set, but I can't help but feel that EMI has screwed the fans once again. I give the album and the music 5 stars, but the box only 3. 1449305172|5.0|As a finance guy turned entrepreneur, I've found the Lean Startup movement revolutionary.  The best thing to happen to entrepreneurship since the demise of indentured servitude!  Ash's 'Running Lean' is the single best, most valuable, usable resource I've found on the subject.<br /><br />I've spent the past 20 years as a student of business strategy and execution.  Since transitioning six years ago from investment management and venture capital to operational roles in technology startups, I knew something was fundamentally missing in startup thinking.  Lean Startup fills the massive gap between my MBA and corporate experience versus the very unique needs in launching and running a startup.  So I let my subscription to Harvard Business Review lapse and have given myself a new MBA in Lean Startup entrepreneurship the past two years.  In 'Running Lean', Ash brings it all together in a way that I've found incredibly valuable.<br /><br />In contrast, Eric Ries's book 'Lean Startup' is light and enjoyable reading that solidly introduces the subject.  This book is serious reading for the serious entrepreneur looking for applied and practical guidance on how to apply the incredibly powerful principles of Lean in their startup.  Make no mistake, it's work.  But if you're serious about launching a startup, 'Running Lean' is the single best guide I've encountered on the subject.

Ash teaches you how to create a one page plan that kicks ass of all those MBA business plans with mission and vision statements and other fluff like that. And even though the lean canvas plan is 1 page only, it is very comprehensive. And actually, it is bloody hard to fill in - as it forces you to think.<br /><br />2. The concept of breaking down risk into 3 parts is epiphany inducing.<br />- Problem risk (is there a problem that needs solving?)<br />- Customer risk (is there demand? Are people willing and able to buy? How can you reach them?)<br />- Market risk (Competition? Will you be able to make a profit?)<br />Ash shows you how to prioritize and tackle each of these risks.<br /><br />3. Running Lean is a real \\"how to\\" book. Most business books are only about \\"what to do\\". They don't tell you how to do stuff.  Running Lean gives you step by step instructions. Ash also gives sample emails and transcripts he uses while dealing with people and validating your idea.<br /><br />4.  Running Lean is chokeful of real life case studies and very helpful graphics. There are quite a few good books out there that have a lot to teach, but because of their boring writing style, they fail to deliver their message. Running Lean thankfully is not one of them. Ash makes his point poignantly.<br /><br />5. Even the Appendix of Running Lean is full of awesome. I can't remember having read the appendix section of any book before. But I devoured the appendix of Running Lean:<br />- How to Build a Low-burn Startup!<br />- How to Achieve Flow in a Lean Startup!<br />- How to Set Pricing for a SaaS product!<br />- How to Get Started With Continuous Deployment!<br />- How to Build Your Conversion Dashboard!<br /><br />Running Lean will prevent you from making a lot of costly mistakes in business.  Read it now because it is one of the best \\"how to\\" business books out there.
